**Skills integrated in this proof**:
1. §1 — natural number induction
2. §2 — peeling off the last term (BigOpAlgebra)
3. §3 — empty sum equals 0 (SummationFormulas)
4. §4 — Pascal's identity (Level 3)
5. §5 — backward rewrite (Level 7)
6. §6 — beyond the edge (Level 8)

**The key insight**: The inductive step uses **Pascal's identity in reverse**.
After peeling and substituting the IH, we have §7,
which is exactly the Pascal expansion of §8. The backward
rewrite §9 folds it back.

The base case uses the **collect-and-close** pattern (from FinsetInduction):
bring §10 into context with §11, then close with a
rewrite. This is the same strategy you'll use whenever a goal has
structural facts that need to be made explicit before the arithmetic
resolves.

**Why "hockey stick"?** In Pascal's triangle, if you highlight a diagonal
sequence of entries and the entry just below the last one (on the opposite
side), the shape looks like a hockey stick:
§12
The bracketed entries show §13. The vertical
sequence is the "stick" and the bottom entry is the "blade."

The second column of Pascal's triangle gives the **triangular numbers**:

§0

Prove the closed-form identity:

§1

This is the multiplicative version of the well-known formula
§2, shifted to avoid natural number subtraction.

**What you need** (all from this world and prior worlds):
- Natural number induction (§3)
- §4 — boundary value C(2, 2) = 1 (Level 1)
- §5 — Pascal's identity (Level 3)
- §6 — reducing C(n, 1) to n (Level 2)
- §7 — distributing multiplication over addition (from BigOpAlgebra: §8)
- Induction hypothesis — substituting the IH
- §9 — closing the final polynomial identity

**Proof plan**:
- **Base case** (§10): C(2, 2) = 1 by §11, then the
  arithmetic closes automatically.
- **Inductive step**: Expand C(n+3, 2) with Pascal's identity, simplify
  C(n+2, 1) = n+2, distribute the factor of 2, substitute the IH,
  then close with §12.

**The pattern**: Peel with Pascal, simplify leaves with the boundary
theorems, distribute, substitute the IH, and close with §8. This
is the **collect-and-close** skeleton (from FinsetInduction): bring
structural facts into context (here via §9), then close with an
algebraic tactic (§10). The same skeleton appears in
SummationFormulas, now applied to binomial coefficients.

**The formula**: §11 counts the number of
2-element subsets — equivalently, the number of handshakes among
§12 people. These are the **triangular numbers**: §13

The triangular numbers are also the partial sums §14 from the SummationFormulas world. In Level 14, you proved this
connection formally: §15. And in Level 15,
you proved the full generalization — the **hockey stick identity**:
§16.

The binomial coefficient §17 also counts the 2-element
subsets of an §18-element set — a connection you'll explore in the
**Powerset** world.

The binomial coefficients also give their name to the **binomial theorem**:
§19
The entries of row §20 of Pascal's triangle are exactly the coefficients
in the expansion of §21 — which is why they are called *binomial*
coefficients. Setting §22 gives §23:
the sum of each row is a power of §24. You'll prove this in the
BinomialTheorem world.

⚠ [1266/1268] Replayed Game.Levels.BinomialCoefficients
warning: Game/Levels/BinomialCoefficients.lean:19:0: No translation (en) found for: Binomial Coefficients
warning: Game/Levels/BinomialCoefficients.lean:21:0: No translation (en) found for: # Binomial Coefficients

A committee of 2 must be chosen from 4 candidates. How many possible
committees are there? And how would you *prove* your answer is correct?

The answer is §0, the **binomial coefficient**.
These numbers have a long history: the arrangement we now call
**Pascal's triangle** was studied by Chinese mathematician Jia Xian
around 1050 CE and by Persian mathematician Omar Khayyam around 1100 CE —
over 500 years before Blaise Pascal published his treatise in 1665.

In this world, you'll learn the Lean formalization §1 and prove
identities using its key properties:

- **Boundary values**: §2, §3, §4
- **Pascal's identity**: §5
- **Symmetry**: §6
- **Beyond the edge**: §7 when §8
- **Factorial formula**: §9
- **Absorption identity**: §10

Pascal's identity is the **recursive heart** of the binomial coefficient.
Combined with the boundary values, it lets you compute any §11
by building Pascal's triangle from the top.

After learning these identities, you'll prove a cross-world connection
(the Gauss sum equals §12), the **hockey stick identity**
§13, and then tackle the
boss: proving by induction that
§14, integrating Pascal's identity
with the peel + IH + ring pattern from the summation worlds.

**Prerequisites**: §15 from SummationFormulas, §16
from FinsetInduction, §17 from BigOpAlgebra, §18 from MeetFin.
